=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Bluffing and outguessing your opponents is just fun. Coup also has inbuilt mechanisms that inevitably force the game to the end quite quickly which is good for games of this category. Player elimination is not a problem either, since that's sole purpose of the game and hence everyone's fine with it. I would gladly play Coup again to fill that 10-15min slot you have.

If I am to give some criticism, would that go for the cheesy and unimaginative art. Furthermore, I am a bit concerned about the longevity of the game. On the other hand it is a good thing to have a very limiten number of characters. On the other hand, a few extra ones could have added to the life span. In additioin, Coup is not a game you would go raving about. It is fun but a bit unrewarding in the long run.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A nice fast little game that we played multiple times at lunch.  Seven games in a row.  All quick.  All cutthroat.  I like the back-stabbery that goes on.

Sadly, I did not win.  But I was quite good at countering any challenges against me.  And I challenged or blocked a few others.  My downfall was usually trying to use my assassination or steal abilities - because even though I had them, the other players always had the abilities to counter them.

Haha!  Played three games in a row and won two of them!  My persistence has paid off!  This time I paid close attention to who could block abilities.  And the last game I was at one card the entire game (lost it in the first round) but still managed to pull through to a win!  Take that!

Now, among my group of friends at lunch, I'm considered quite a threat in this game, and they usually attempt to knock me out early - not that they succeed, but it's cute how they try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
A party game that is much weaker in gameplay than werewolf.

The 'power' of these cards are interesting and in a way drives the game, where players do their guess work and take that chance.

It's really only a matter of time that you will make the 'attack' accusation, as the cards are running out. In the end, it's usually the one that outlast everyone, not because he was good, but it was the others that was bad. It could just be pure luck that by the time it was your turn, the obvious was already at hand. With such minimal cards left,is there really a joy in making that guess?

Much like playing a guessing game that eventually comes down to a 50-50 chance. Might as well begin with that. It would have been a faster game then.

All is not lost, if your group of friends are new gamers and felt there was a strategy in hiding their card's identity, as being fun.

Can't share the joy from a old timer wargamer.

I feel like the game is built to be fast, to deal with the problem of player elimination. I want a bigger, better Coup. I'm not sure what game that is.

RATING: 8
This game assuredly breaks the rule against player elimination, but it does so within the context of a very brief game, and an otherwise very enjoyable experiment in human interaction.

  The social aspect of the game, and the possible alliance building possibilities in a 5 or 6 player game give this game the definite ability to please a lot of people and non-gamer partygoers who are willing to sit down and play a short game for the heck of it.  

Once they grasp the card interactions, chances are just about everyone will find something to like in "the experience" of playing at least three hands of this game.

Reminds me a little bit of Nanuk in that this game manages to create a party game out of very little...though this game is MUCH better than Nanuk.

RATING: 10
This is one of my favorite games to play with five or six people. The rounds are quick, and when people really get into it, it's an absolute blast.

I've lost count of the times I've heard friends - and new strangers at conventions - say things like "As the Duke of Duke-ington, I will collect my taxes from you poor fools," only to be countered by someone saying "Ah ha, good sir, but, you see, I am the Duke of Duke-ington, and I do not believe you hold that title!"

It's fun. You should play it. The rules take some explaining, but any questions are usually cleared up by the end of the first round.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A great hidden role, bluffing game that works well in any size group with both gamers and non-gamers. While in most hidden role games, you are forced to play as the character you are dealt whether you like it or not, in this game, it doesn't even matter what you are dealt because you can play as whatever you want. The game is as interesting as you choose to make it. You choose how risky you want to play and you create the tension.

Easy to teach with simple rules; has player elimination but plays fast.

RATING: 9
Brilliant social filler game you can teach to people in minutes. 

Great for all kinds of players, loaded with intrigue, back stabbing, vicious cut throat play but over in moments so no bad blood.

Fast, super portable, easy to each, set up, great art/universe creation, fun bluffing game with the expansion supports up to 10 players.

Social/bluffing game that engages even non social players as cards detailing the roles/actions (or claimed roles/actions) allows for gameplay to run smoothly and support social interaction.

Really removes a lot of the barriers or pitfalls for other social games like werewolf and needs less table management, making it way more fun in my opinion.

RATING: 9
Little card game, just 15 cards.
Each player has a influence of 2 cards. Through actions, bluffing, challenges and blocking other players you try to be the last player remaining with influence.

The possible actions:

1 - receive 1 coin - no blocking possible.
2 - receive 2 coins - can be blocked by any player that has (or claims to have) a Duke.
3 - coup - pay 7 coins and make a player discard face up a influence card. If that player now has 2 cards face up they are out of the game. - no blocking possible.

Character actions (all can be challenged, some can be blocked) -

The challenge comes before blocking, but blocking can also be challenged. 

1 Duke - receive 3 coins - anyone can challenge this player to prevent him taking the action (if they think that that player is bluffing).
2 Assassin - pay 3 coins to make a player lose (discard face up) a card. Can be challenged, after a possible challenge the defending player can block claiming they have the Contessa - this can also be challenged.
3 Captain - steals 2 coins from any player - plays like the Assassin but this time can be blocked by a Captain or Embassador.
4 Embassador - draws 2 cards and adds them to his 2 or 1 (if already lost a influence) and chooses 2 to discard. This can be challenged but not blocked.
5 - Contessa - doesn't do anything, her only purpose is to defend against the Assassin - blocking him. A defending player that claims to have the Contessa can also be challenged.

A challenge goes like this:

The challenged player proves (reveal the card) he has the card he claims to have, if he does have then the challenger loses a influence, and you carry out your action (and shuffle the challenged card back in the deck and draw a new one), if the challenged player doesn't have the card he loses an influence (reveal one of your 2 cards), and the action associated with that character is canceled.

RATING: 6.5
Complots is a 5-10 minutes filler about bluffing.
It's a simple, fun, barebone bluffing game, with no chrome added.

Each players starts with two cards, hidden, which also are its two life points.
On its turn, the player can perform one of eight actions. Three are basic, the others are associated with one of the five characters. One can claim to have any character (cards are hidden) and therefore perform any action. But if the bluff is called and the player doesn't have the character, he must lose one life (discarding one card); if it has the character, the other player loses the life.

It's a clever design, a game made of just a few cards and some tokens, and I definitely look forward to play it again!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game has a great concept.  It reminds me of playing B.S. where you can freely lie all you want, and it's not until someone challenges you that you have to prove if you're telling the truth.  The different roles all have their advantages, however I still think there are a couple that are a little weaker.  Of course the brilliance of the game is that it doesn't matter if you have the weaker roles because you can lie and pretend you have the stronger roles.  As you can see your aptitude at this game will directly correlate with your ability to lie convincingly, although a well-placed truth can be just as effective.  It's a quick game, and uses a very limited number of components to create a decent amount of fun.  The only reason I don't love it is that the gameplay requires a group of players that I don't have in my family.  People that enjoy lying as a game mechanism will have the most fun with Coup.

RATING: 7.25
My group likes playing this for some light-hearted deception. For whatever reason, it tends to bring out an extremely goofy delight in us all as we become these over-the-top characters exuberantly proclaiming the exploits of "Oooooh, the Double Duke!", flamboyantly blocking a Captain's stealing as... THE CAPTAIN, cooly thwarting assassination attempts with Contessa, and more! Or are those all blatant lies?!?! I generally don't prefer the game to other things since I find it pretty shallow, but it's great fun for turning us into wacky characters. And I do love introducing this to non-gamers and those very new to the hobby. It's always solid fun for a little while. I think my ideal player counts are 4 and 5. Any more and I'd rather be playing Resistance: Avalon or One Night Ultimate Werewolf.

RATING: 6.5
Decent quick party game.

A purely bluffing strategy game.  There are several types of characters.  You have two of them.  Each can take certain actions.  Each player takes turns making actions that they may be able, or not able to do, and it is up to other players to call them out if they think they are bluffing.  There's some other mechanics involved, but that's the primary thrust of the game.

Look at my Wishlist for things I would trade for.

RATING: 10
Will forever be one of my go to games. Play is simple, games are fast, gives a wonderful mix of strategy, luck, and social play. 

You can perform a number of actions depending on the role cards you have, but role cards are hidden so you can lie and pretend to be any and all roles. People can call your bluff and then you lose a life, but if you're telling the truth they die instead. 

Coup Reformation is an expansion that I always play with. 

For completely new roles but the same gameplay look at Coup Rebellion G54

Tutorial Video: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rLA72SoIhOU

RATING: 7.5
A fast (~20 min) bluffing game that was a lot more fun than I thought it would be. Players are assigned two role cards faced down, but can claim they have any role when taking an action associated with a role. The other players can call the bluff. If you lied you loose one of your roles, if you were telling the truth they accuser loses one. Last person standing wins.

RATING: 7
A cross between Love Letter and The Resistance. This is a fun little game. It's very light and airy, but so easy to play and so quick (five minutes for a hand even) that the return on investment is very satisfying. There's a pretty good chance each hand that you'll have some laugh worthy moments mixed in with a few moments in which you have to attack someone without a good reason as to whom. Like Cosmic Encounter, your group has to add a little social energy into the game to get it to sing. For the price and investment, you really can't go wrong if you want to squeeze a diverting social game into a small amount of time; I recommend Coup for that reason. I have played with three and four and now won't play with fewer than four. I have yet to play with The Reformation expansion but I anticipate the very small tweak it offers to give the game that little bit more richness that I feel it needs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I can see how this would be a good, perhaps great, game in the right company.  I did not play with those people.  

Fundamentally, the game is uninteresting if everyone tells the truth.  The real game is in determining who is lying.  However, the stakes are a little too high for the person calling the bluff - if you call someone and are wrong, then you lose 1 of 2 cards.  This means that the people I played with were very hesitant to call someone unless it was obvious they were lying.  Everyone was also hesitant to lie, so the game kind of played itself.  And it was boring.

Now, I admit up front that this is more about the players than the game.  However, I do not think these were atypical players.  That is, I think being hesitant to lie and hesitant to call others will be typical, especially for first time players, unless you're talking about a poker group, or a group that is used to bluffing being part of the game.  It is therefore critical for the person teaching the game to stress that this is a game about bluffing.

RATING: 5
I think maybe it just occupies too weird of a niche for me. It lacks the nail-biting tension of a full-fledged bluffing game, and also lacks the sheer lighthearted fun factor of a good filler/microgame. There's no real laughs here.

This game just feels... flawed. It's short enough that I enjoy it well enough well playing, I just don't know that I would really suggest it. Firstly, bluffing only really seems viable early game. I like the feel of the risk when you challenge someone early game, but the penalty if you're wrong is just SO harsh. Once you're down to one card, your plays just feel incredibly limited.

The real problem I have with this game, though, is the endgame always feels incredibly anticlimactic. Every game we've played things becomes increasingly deterministic, and the end just boils down to mathing out enough money to win. I'm used to bluffing games like Liar's Dice, or hell, even poker, where the final play is when tensions are at their highest. This feels like the opposite, and that's coming from a group that LOVES bluffing games!

I suspect 4 is the ideal number here. With more hidden information, there's more room to bluff, and it's less likely that one person will spend half the game with only one card while everyone else sits around with both of theirs.

RATING: 5
While it’s not a particularly good game per se, I’m not sure about its merits as a filler – it might actually be okay. At least that is my first impression.

Yes, there is a lot of bluffing involved and almost no deduction imo, and yes it’s quite random, but considering it takes 5-10 minutes max to play the game is actually not that bad. Admitted, there isn’t really much to the game – it’s all about the bluffing! And I guess that can be fun sometimes, but it’s not like you feel you played much better or worse than the other players if you win or lose… Though I have to say that there is still room for some clever play/bluffing. 

Coup is almost always compared to Love Letter, and for what it’s worth I think it’s slightly better than Love Letter. In LL you're restricted to your two cards, so the luck of the draw plays a slightly bigger role; yes, you also draw 2 random cards in Coup, but you can lie about which cards you have! This makes Coup a little less random (I think) and more fun because of the bluffing. 

As Coup only takes 5-10 minutes to play I won’t refuse to play it, and I would gladly play it a few times, but I would rather play a filler like No Thanks or For Sale.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]Includes Coup: Reformation kickstarter edition.[/b]

Coup is a rapid-quick, 15 card game of lying and bluff. The closest example to it that I have is Liar's Dice. But instead of dice values hidden in a cup, you can choose from five different character powers, brazenly taking one of six options whether you have the card or not. You've got two cards. If someone catches you in the act, you have to flip a card up. Lose both, and you're out. It's very fast and efficient.

As far as card fillers go, there are more "thinky" or challenging options. But if you want a game that really distills out the feeling of poker and shameless lying and bluff, then this is a pretty solid option.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Bluffing and social deduction games can be fun, but they aren't my first choice in games since I am horrible at them. This one was pretty easy to pick up as you have 2 roles and 7 options to choose from on your turn. On your turn you take one of the actions and you can potentially call someone's bluff when they take an action. Last one standing wins. I'm happy it plays in 15 minutes because that's about as much fun as I can get out of it before I'm either ready to move on to something else or call it a night. Playing both this and Sheriff of Nottingham in the same week I would rather play this simply due to the playtime but again only if we had a random 15-20 minutes at the start or end of a game night.

RATING: 8

It is a good game for gamers and non-gamers as it is short and easy to understand. There are five possible roles (cards) while each player has only two....but can claim and perform the action of any of the five roles. Players choose to call their bluff, or not as it may mean losing one of their own cards if the opponent is not bluffing. Full of laughs.

RATING: 6
Coup hits most of the sweet spots that I look for in a game of bluff, the most obvious ones being opportunities for bluff-counter bluff plays and build in mechanisms to keep the game from dragging out too much.

Basically you start the game with 2 special powers and a small menu of basic actions that are much worse. On your turn you can take one of the basic actions, or play one of your 2 cards face down, declare what card it is, and take a special action associated with the declared card, of which there are 5. The rub is that you do not have to reveal until someone challenges you, saying that either you are lying, or that they have a particular card that counters yours so you can hypothetically be taking actions for cards you don't have as long as no one calls you out on it. Cards are revealed in challenges, and the lying player who is called out gets punished by losing one of their cards, this applies both to the active player, or the player who challenges (if they are challenging by stating they have one of the cards that counters the one that the active player used). 

This challenge mechanism is central to the game, as losing both cards is how you lose the game. To further push the speed of the game it also has a timer mechanism where you can slowly tick up your money at the rate of at least 1 coin a round, with 7 coins being usable to take 1 card away from a player of your choice. Alternatively, there is also a role that allows you to speed that up considerably, as it only costs 3 coins to do the same thing, but again, is reliant upon having the card or successfully bluffing that you have the card.

This is a bit gamey, as the description might make apparent, but the game actually flows pretty well once you get into it and see how it functions. I would be more than willing to play this readily with an experienced group as filler between games. It is pretty stripped down, but still has some fun room for play within its system.

RATING: 8.5
Coup is a quick bluffing card game.  I have only played with 4 and 6 players so far.  Liked it a little better with 4 - with 6 it takes a while to get back to your turn again, and I'd rather play the Resistance :).

Games play in about 20 minutes, so even if you are eliminated, it's not for too long.  Lot's of fun opportunities to bluff, and it's always a good time when someone gets called out, whether they were bluffing or not!  

Also, all of my friends seem to like it better than the Resistance - it is nice to be able to choose whether or not you bluff (as opposed to the Resistance where it is assigned), and there are more options in general in Coup - should I bluff the Duke from the start of the game?  Should I bluff contessa if I am assassinated, or should I call out the other person, or should I just lose a card?  Surprising depth for such a quick (and quick-to-teach) game!

RATING: 6
Coup feels like another in a long line of recent microgames which have become popular.  It's most reminiscent of Love Letter, but also carries forward the theme of The Resistance.

Coup is essentially a bluffing game where you're trying to outwit your opponent, but it lacks substance.  It makes up for this in a short playtime (or very short if you're doing 2-player).

Thumbs up for good art, portability, and that it's fast.  I just didn't feel like there is much to it beyond that.  Happy to play it if offered, but one I need to own.

RATING: 5
Quite a fun light bluffing game. The synergy of the five roles is what makes this game interesting: they're all useful, and players will usually feel a need to have access to all of them throughout the game. The near-indispensability of all roles and the quickly changing game state means players' cannot change their roles fast enough to keep up with the needs of the moment, creating a pressure to bluff, which makes the game tense.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Someone once told me, "The Resistance is a deduction game with some bluffing; Coup is a bluffing game with some deduction." This seemed more or less spot-on after a play or two. I'm decent at bluffing inside of systems I'm familiar with, or when lying is basically mandatory due to role-draw or whatnot, but tend to have an honest streak when it's my choice whether to deceive. This makes this game difficult for me - but also really good practice. :)

If it were any longer I'd find the player elimination annoying, but if players don't get too hung up on things it plays quite quickly.

ETA: After a dozen plays or so, I'm finding that games of this are rarely as interesting / dynamic as I'd hope that they would be. There's rarely enough information in the system for deduction to be relevant, and the game seems to reward honesty nearly as much as bluffing. (Or best of all, being honest while seeming like you're bluffing.) Some friends tell me that [gameid=139] is a similar sort of game which has much better dynamics; I'll have to give that a try sometime.

RATING: 8
The replay value is somewhat limited in the same group, but a game or three of Coup is a great way to get a game night started. It's easy to teach, the strategies are simple to grasp while still having some depth to discover, and each game is fast enough to dull the pain of an early elimination. That said, there's a lot of luck and "gang up on the leader" is the order of the day, so it's welcome will wear out pretty quick with the same group.

Players represent Italian families during the Renaissance, wielding power and influence.  There are 15 cards in the game, 3 each of the Duke, Captain, Contessa, Assassin and Ambassador.  These five types of cards each grant a special power in the game.  Two of these cards are dealt to each player, face down, and are ideally kept hidden throughout the game.  On a turn, a player chooses an action from a choice of eight, the five special powers above or three common powers.  The trick here is you can bluff - you may be holding the Duke, allowing you to take 3 coins, or you may not - but you can still claim you are taking the Duke's action and take 3 coins.  Any player can challenge your action, and if you're not holding the card allowing that action, you flip the card up - otherwise you reveal and replace it, and the challenger flips one of their cards face up.  If you have both cards flipped up, you're out of the round.  Last one standing wins.  

This is a fun game of bluff and deduction, very similar in pace, size and fun as the recent Love Letter.  Players have to carefully monitor the cash flowing around the table, as a player who reaches 7 coins can call a Coup - an unblockable attack that forces a player to reveal a card.  Games only take 10 minutes, and are a lot of fun.  A little gem.

After a year and hundreds of games, of course it's a 10.  Perfection.

RATING: 4
Coup is weird. You have a strong negative feedback loop; if you get hit first, you have less stuff to work with. This would seem to make you treat the game as a light take-that affair; who cares if you get knocked out?

On the other hand, the endgame can be super mathy and interesting and complex. The game doesn't seem to hang together for me.

I gather that the skill is coming out of the early part of the game with good stuff to win the math at the end, but the multiplayer chaos seems to make that difficult to impossible. And looking in the forums for the game, a lot of high-level strategy seems to be hanging back so that people don't pick on you, which is super boring in a game this simple. I don't get it.

RATING: 6
Bit of fun, but most rounds are pretty simple, depending on how much thought you put in.

If you just take it at face-value, it's a bit of a random game. You'll find yourself suddenly being unable to act without being taken out, or unable to make any actions without being countered.

Gets substantially better if you go in with a plan - what you're going to bluff, and when. Pick a story and stick to it from the start, and you'll tend to do fairly well. The fun then comes in from making your strategy work when things go wrong. Even then, games are a little too short to do too much with it. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Poker for the 21st Century.  This game is a work of genius. Only five characters, all created to equally counterweight each other.  It can be played strictly based on strategy or you can ‘bluff.’  I’m usually not a fan of bluffing games, but this one seems perfect as you actually don’t have to bluff to win.  There is strategy here, especially when played with the “Reformation” expansion.  For new players, I recommend one round of the base game to understand each of the characters’ abilities, and then jump right into the expansion “Reformation.”  
Regarding downsides: You really need four or five players; any more or less can cause issues, although six is good when working in ‘teams’ with the “Reformation” expansion (because when players start out they are only focused on the three players on the ‘opposing’ team).  Player elimination is here, but made up for by the short game play (just like Poker).   
In a similar marketing tactic as “Love Letter”, I really wish they offered this game in more of a variety of re-themed sets to the general market (and not just limited-edition convention exclusives).  This game is great for non-gamers, but if we had more themes to choose from, we could tailor the set to the particular audience we are introducing it to.  Also, at this point, Coup and its expansion “Coup: Reformation” really should be packaged together as it only adds a few more cards to an already scant deck, but I suspect the publisher gets more returns on profit by selling them separately.

RATING: 7.5
Quick & simple hidden role card game. Strong bluffing element.

Elegant is the word here - the game cards are Duke, Captain, Ambassador, Assassin, Contessa and that's it. Keep your cards alive by keeping them face down, lie about what they are and try not to get caught out.

This game is stupid popular. Most people I've played it with love it.

There is one small issue we've encountered with the game. There's a mechanism where any player, not just the one you're interacting with, can call you out on a bluff. Calling a bluff is risky - if they can satisfy it, you lose a life instantly. So no one ever calls a bluff if it's not their turn.

Maybe there's a house rule that can fix it? Otherwise this is a pretty decent game, only made worse because Coup G54 is available and better in almost every way.

RATING: 8
Coup builds a fulfilling experience out of the bluff and call. Coup demands players lie to, doubt, and accuse each other to win. As the rulebook says, “there is no second place”. If your favorite part of playing Battlestar Galactica is yelling “CYLON!” at everyone, you’ll love Coup.

Coup plays lightning-quick. Games take only 5–10 minutes on average. The cards are quite nice. The art is distinct and very cool. My personal favorite touch is the cards’ stretched, portrait aspect ratio. The box is adorably tiny. With just the core components, Coup is downright pocketable. Perfect travel game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is a really quick and simply card game composed of only 15 cards.  There are 5 cards with 3 copies of each.  The game is played with each player taking a turn until only one person has survived.  Each player will be dealt 2 cards which will be placed facedown after being looked at by their respective player.  Your facedown cards are your hit points.  

On your turn, you can choose to do a non-character action: which is acquiring money or spending 7 money to make an opponent lose 1 HP by having them flip a card of their choice.  Non-character actions are safe (ie can't lose HP) to the players that are performing the action.  You can perform any one of the 4 different character actions you want.  If you do, someone can accuse you of not-having that character among your two cards.  If they are right you lose an HP by flipping a character of your choice.  If they are wrong, you prove it by showing them the character and they flip one of their characters instead.  After proving you have the character, you shuffle it into the deck and draw a new card.

Some non-character and character actions can be blocked by saying that you have a particular character card.  Again, people can challenge you if people think you're lying.  Characters abilities focus on acquiring money, getting new character cards, making people lose HP (cheaper than the non-character action), and blocking the loss of HP from character actions.  The game is pretty quick and has really easy rules.  It should be a good filler for groups that like bluffing and interaction. Better than Love Letter.

I no longer own any games that have player elimination, so that's affected my enjoyment of this game.

RATING: 7
Coup
Pros
--Lying, deception, and deduction in a very pure form.
--Entirely different strategies can lead to dominance
--Fits in almost any available time slot, including teaching the rules
Cons
--Too short, to the point of being able to take any amount of risk with little consequence, as the “next round” is less than 5 minutes away
--Not a satisfying 2 player game; needs at least 3
--Getting any of the cards marked or damaged can drastically diminish the experience, moreso than most other games

RATING: 8
Coup is one of those few games that I would describe as the perfect micro-game.

Amusingly, this game hinges on one concept, is this person full of **** when it comes to their hand?  Or are they telling the truth?  Because ultimately players are either holding the card they say, or they don't, but how much are you willing to be on it?  Granted, there are elements of deduction and currency to stop the game from hitting a stand-still but this isn't a game that overstays its welcome, it is just long enough and people are always keen to play it.

Let the finger pointing and accusations begin!

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is a fun light social deduction game.  The other game people play in this genre is Love Letter.  I much prefer Coup to Love Letter for the following reasons:

1).  Love Letter is a bait and switch.  They tell you "You'll get to guess what people have!" but the game is too short to narrow what you think opponents may have beyond 2 or 3 roles.

2).  Coup has a bluffing mechanic.  You can leave false trails and traps for people.  People will fall for them because they have an actual reason to; there is proper deduction here.

3).  Rather than taking 3 minutes, it takes a proper 15.  15 minutes per game allows for genuine gotcha moments.  Love Letter ultimately *feels* like filler, and I cannot remember what happened after the fact.

So, why does this only get a 6?  After repeat play, it gets rather stale.  There are few deep strategies here.  The winner is almost entirely random.  While there are good moments, the game itself is lackluster.

RATING: 9
I used this game to introduce non board game players to the board game world. The cards wore out so quickly because people kept playing them.

Pros:
1. Very fast and easy to explain especially with the game's player's reference card, and give it a whirl whilst explaining.
2. It only takes < 10 mins.
3. Deceptively simple, but very enjoyable when it goes down to bluffing and calling bluffs.
4. Beautiful art and cast of characters.

Cons:
1. May seem unfair and broken if everyone abuses the duke powers -->
2. Because of this, house rules may or may not be required for a better game play in your specific gaming group.(But we enjoy doing this so maybe it's a good thing?)

Very recommended for gaming groups that are:
1. New to gaming
2. More of a tactical person rather than strategical(Strategy works better in Avalon)
3. Wants a quick game that can be repeated over and over again.

Not recommended for:
1. People who dislike direct competition ("I am going to assassinate you, right now :D")
2. People who prefer more long-term strategy games
3. People who hate lying/bluffing (It is very required to do so to have fun and or win)

RATING: 2
Not so much a hidden identity deduction game as a 'have a wild guess as to who I am game', as the hidden identities change constantly and are shuffled into and out of the deck, regardless of whether you guess correctly or not.

There is absolutely no reason for you to ever bluff as you will lose your card and the card abilities aren't good enough to warrant the risk.

Only played a single game but it seemed the player that won was the player who did not participate, i.e. never accused anyone, never trying to block any one etc, etc. 

I like the idea of having two 'lives' as it were however this game is about as much fun as guessing whether the next card in a standard deck is red or black.

RATING: 8
If there's a game that can get in the ring with Love Letter to fight for the "King of the Fillers" title, it's Coup.

The rules are simple, the choices/bluffs are exhilarating, and the counter choices/bluffs are risky.  Absolutely fantastic mix of risk/reward.

Like Love Letter, even if you're knocked out quickly, each game only last 10-15 minutes, so you won't be waiting long.

I highly recommend getting the Reformation expansion and using the inquisitors and allegiance cards as soon as you can.  The replacement role and extra layer of allegiances is perfect.

RATING: 8
Probably falls into the gamers game trap. Super fun but maybe not for the random intros - prob can only get good if you spend the mental energy to learn all the characters as opposed to one night.

Edit after many plays: I've had ~10 sessions of this game with multiple plays each. People range from very quickly grasping to taking awhile.

Reactions have run from ravenous fandom to lukewarm. I think the reason for this is that the game is very focused on strategic bluffing (different than the bluffing in skulls for example but I'm having trouble articulating why - I guess because the bluffing here has a bit of "engine building" elements where skulls is just pure people reading). If you like that you'll like this game. If you don't you won't. 

Decisive, but is so quick and "efficient" (meaning everything a player does is important to be paying attention to and nothing is fiddly) that it's way more likely than not to be a hit with a group of gamers, even if one person isn't quite vibing it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
While set in the same universe as The Resistance, and containing deduction and hidden roles, Coup is much closer to a game of BS than a game of Werewolf. Players each receive two hidden roles (out of a possible five). Each role provides a specific powerful action to the player. A player's turn simply consists of taking one of these actions. 

The catch is that a player can take any action they choose, regardless of what roles they actually possess. It then becomes incumbent on the other players to decide whether or not to call the bluff. If a bluff is called, either the caller or the call-ee will lose one of their roles. It stays face up on the table for all to see. If a player loses both his roles, he is eliminated from the game. 

Games take under 15 minutes to play and provide some wonderful group dynamics. I like the moment that happens when someone takes the perfect action for the situation and no one at the table believes it but no one wants to call them on it either, so they just get to do it while everyone else looks at each other disgustedly. It almost has to be played multiple times in a row as players learn both the interactions of the cards themselves as well as their interactions with each other. This is a great example of a game that plays much "bigger" than the rules or components would suggest; the game is really all about the people playing it. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
ESSEN 2012

+ Fast

First couple of plays I hated it... then I came to think it's ok

Now I bought it and I'm kinda not liking it so much any more.

It's a light game, easy enough for non gamers, small enough to bring it to the pub and fast enough that you can get plenty of games.

Mark hit the nail on the head with what I dislike about it being the player elimination aspect - even if you go down to one card early you're basically also eliminated on the rationale that you can't really come back from there and your choices are limited because of it.

I guess the flip point to that is that at least the rounds are fast enough that player elimination isn't an issue and it also does depend on the crowd you're playing with - like for instance if it's a pub/social thing

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
It can be described as a combination of Love Letter and Skull & Roses (or Liar's Dice).  Basically you have two cards and can choose an action that is either one of 3 universal actions or one associated with 4 of the 5 different cards in the deck (3 copies each of 5 different cards = 15 cards in the deck).  The trick is you might be bluffing and using an ability from a card you don't possess... and anybody can call you out on it if they don't believe you have the goods.  If you were bluffing, you permanently lose a card.  If not, the person who called you out permanently loses one.  Or everyone can take your word for it and move on.  Some cards also have counter-effects, and those can be challenged as well. Very quick and intense game.  Best of all, Coup (best with 4-6 players) fits in nicely between Love Letter (2-4 players) and The Resistance (best with 7-10 players).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.25
This game really took me by surprise.  I resisted being persuaded to play, but I am glad that I relented.  This is a very fast game and it lends itself well to being played repeatedly.  It has a very elegant, well balanced, and simple design.  The game truly shines in the player interaction department.  Players interact with each other intensively.  Players are rewarded for both lying effectively as well as catching others in lies.  While I loathe the games that assign players on teams and they must figure out who is lying like Bang and its ugly kin, I don't mind all the titillating mendacity in this particular game because everyone is for themselves and everyone is lying.  Or are they?  I know this is a good game because I am looking forward to playing again and I am confident it will be fun.

RATING: 6
Coup is a bluffing role playing game. The game shows last man standing politics. Coup combines various game mechanics together, for example, bluffing, role playing, various player powers, deduction and etc. For this reason, before playing game, players need to be skilled to practice characters first. After couple of plays, I still feel like the game tends to be playing Truth or Dare. According to the comparison between Coup with Mascarade by many boardgamers, for me, I prefer Mascarade more. In my opinion, Mascarade needs a little easier role playing skill to players. Moreover, Mascarade provides bigger accommodation from 2 to 13 players. Mascarade is more suitable for party size game. Anyway, Coup is also a nice bluffing game. The game provides short and fast pace progression.

RATING: 5
An alright game, though I have a sneaking suspicion it's not going to hold up to multiple plays. When you get right down to it, it's essentially the card game "bulls**t" but with fancy clothing. 

Yeah, there are roles. Yeah, there are coins (which really are just a secondary end-game trigger). But ultimately the game comes down to one thing: calling people out for lying. 

It was pretty good fun, and it was thankfully a good length for what the game offers. However, I feel that there is no possibility for any actual deduction to take place in this game. It's all being able to read people and/or get lucky guessing. And if I'm playing that kind of a game, it would seem like normal bulls**t is a much more elegant and fun game to play, without any player elimination.

RATING: 7

2013-04-15

Initial Rating: 8.0 (April 2013)

What a great simple game. I really like Love Letter but Coup is in another category altogether, way better :thumbsup:. 

The game can be explained under 5 minutes and it takes around 12 minutes to complete a play so I don’t mind the player elimination and/or the blind guesses. It only has 15 cards.

In general, the game is pretty similar in mechanics to Love Letter, however I like the bluffing needed (much more prevalent in this game as you can lie, this is why I prefer it over Love Letter) and hidden roles is my favorite mechanic.

The production values are very well done, the game offers a very clear aid to each player and I dig the art.

I don’t have anything bad to say about Coup, in fact it has the potential to go up in my ratings. If you like bluffing buy this ASAP. Coup is a must have for me.

Current rating: 8.0

2016-02-21

I no longer believe that  Coup: Guatemala 1954 (or   Coup: Rebellion G54) is a better edition of the game. They are just different games (with the same core rules) but sometimes I crave playing the original Coup over the others. Coup: Guatemala 1954 adds complexity to a very simple game and sometimes I´m in the mood for that as well. I like both games and I have both in my game collection.
 
Current Rating: 7.0

RATING: 8
In general I don't like fillers.  I don't even care for most games less than an hour, so a 5-minute game isn't normally going to get considered.

Coup, however, is simple and beautiful.  It's Liar's Dice with no math harder than counting to 10, a straightforward bluffing game which plays incredibly well assuming the players have a good sense of humor.  I played 9 hands at BGG.CON and had a blast.

I'll be P&Ping this one now, and buying an official copy whenever they get wide distribution in the USA.

RATING: 8
My rating here reflects my impression after only two plays. I suspect it will take more for my opinion to really solidify, and I even think this rating will probably go up. The game is a bit like Mascarade, but the chaos is slightly more controlled. The strategy is not immediately obvious, but even after two plays, I am beginning to see some depths worth exploring.

Update: I have more than 20 plays under my belt now and this game is definitely better than my initial impression. It may take a few plays for the group to get into the bluffing and challenging mindset, but once they do, this game is a blast. It plays fast, so the player elimination isn't a horrible thing. My group loves it.

RATING: 8.2
It's not often that I enjoy games of bluff or games of elimination, but Coup makes both mechanics very enjoyable.  Limiting players to only a handful of decisions per turn makes it easy to remember what to do so, after a couple plays, bluffing is easier since you don't have to constantly check your reference card.  Also, making each of those turns start and end quickly, even if you are out immediately, you're never out very long.  This is a very interesting and enjoyable game that plays in 5 or 10 minutes and can be played multiple times without exhausting the game itself.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A short filler game that involves some deduction and lots of bluffing. Players are all attempting to rid competitors of their influence in court, (their lifes), by taking actions such as stealing money, taxing, and assainating. Each players begins the game with 2 characters (Duke, Captain, Assasin, Ambassador and Contessa), and play continues until only player still has "influence" in court. You may take the actions indicated by the cards you possess, or you may claim to play an action reserved to a character you do not have in your hand (bluffing). In either case, you may take that action unless someone calls your bluff. If you indeed were bluffing, then you will lose a life, but if you weren't bluffing, then the challenger will lose a life.

Great game. Only takes about 15 mins to play which is great, but it is still jam-packed with strategy and action. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Free-for-all hidden role card game with bluffing and chaos. More decisions than Love Letter, less chaos than Mascarade, both of which are good things. There seemed to be more freedom in the early game, though, and endgames can drag out when bluffing and challenging are no longer viable options. The best way to win is to never have to bluff.

RATING: 8.5
Fun, fast, sometimes epic, generates lots of great moments, sometimes frustrating but you know from the get go that this is not a fair playing field. The game evolved as we learned counter and counter-bluffing, and how to use each role fully.

This is probably our favorite party/filler game in my collection, an instant classic. Usually play from 5 to 8 (w/ expansion), the base game is still very good, but the Inquisitor addition in the extension makes for a much better game overall, as the Ambassador does adds has much bluffing potential - it's rare to contest an Ambassador call.

Only caveat is that the last few turns with 2 (sometimes 3) remaining players can be a bit predictable if roles are known (it,s hard to bluff at this stage unless you have an ambassador/just drew some new cards). Does not diminish the rest of the game for us.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In the debate over which microgame is better, Coup or Love Letter, I'm firmly in the Coup camp. I don't dislike Love Letter, but Coup is the game I want to play again.

I have introduced this to at least 25 people. Almost everyone has liked it and preferred it to Love Letter and even, for a surprising number of people, The Resistance. Given the range of people I've introduced this to, their enthusiastic response, and high demand for the new Resistance-themed version, this could become a modern classic and a staple of many collections.

For me, Coup rates an 8 and is trending up. Because it's a bluffing game the experience depends a great deal on the people at the table. With people who get this game this is a great game.

One downside is that with five roles, after enough plays with the same group, certain patterns will emerge. This is where Coup: Guatemala 1954 comes in. It has the same structure as Coup but comes with 25 different roles. You choose five roles for the game and go from there. Excellent!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I have the original City State edition as well as this one, the Resistance themed edition with new artwork. 

This new edition should make Coup the hit it deserves to be. I've been playing it regularly for over a year and have introduced it to several dozen people. Most people have liked it a great deal, and many have wanted their own copy. Now they can finally get one.

See my notes on the original edition for additional comments.

RATING: 10
One of the best "filler" games on the market. Extremely quick games filled with treachery, lying and back-stabbing, much like real life politics. You don't feel bad when you're eliminated because a new game will likely be starting in mere moments. Totally enjoyable for master bluffers and honest do-gooders alike. Always a blast when it hits the table, and it hits the table often.

RATING: 7.7
Fun social deduction game that plays in less than 10 min per round.  Everyone has a role that allows them to do an action against or in defense of anyone else. But you can also just lie and say that you have a role, that is until someone calls you out on it! Mix between the older card game "BS" and games like resistance/werewolf. Dead simple to learn, play setup is easy, and the game is cheap. Always a good ice breaker or time killer.

RATING: 9
Coup is basically dystopian sci-fi poker, in a number of ways. The games are quite short, usually, and the entire play of the game is about bluffing. This game is best enjoyed casually among friends, preferably with alcohol involved. It's a fairly simple game that takes very little time to explain, and the opportunities the game allows for one-on-one stare-downs about particular moves (is he/she bluffing, or not?) can be a great laugh for everyone at the table. Highly, highly recommended.

RATING: 4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Have via the Kickstarter version of Reformation.

Coup's art is just brilliant, portraying a blend of fantastical sci-fi like Coruscant in Phantom Menace or The Capital in Hunger Games.

The theme is ultimately pasted on to a tidy little game of bluffing and intrigue, where you can do anything you'd like whenever you'd like unless someone challenges you and it turns out your lying. In that event, you lose an influence as well as flip a card face up. When you run out of influence or face down cards, you're out. You only start with two cards face down, and there's no way to get more (although there are several methods of exchanging).

The upside of this is the downside - it encourages you to play fast and loose with both your bluffs and your challenges, because each time you're caught or wrong you're 50% out of the game on card flips even if you have tons of influence.

If that plays too fast for you or dis-incents your crowd from doing any lying, for smaller games you could say it's two influence to cause a flip and just drop an influence coin on a card to track its status. This would encourage both more bluffing and and more challenging and would actually add a second layer of bluffing, since people could lie about losing a challenge to confuse people but still take the influence hit. I'll try it next time I get past a few rounds.

RATING: N/A
In Coup, you want to be the last player with influence in the game, with influence being represented by face-down character cards in your playing area.

Each player starts the game with two coins and two influence – i.e., two face-down character cards; the fifteen card deck consists of three copies of five different characters, each with a unique set of powers:
• Duke: Take three coins from the treasury. Block someone from taking foreign aid.
• Assassin: Pay three coins and try to assassinate another player's character.
• Contessa: Block an assassination attempt against yourself.
• Captain: Take two coins from another player, or block someone from stealing coins from you.
• Ambassador: Draw two character cards from the Court (the deck), choose which (if any) to exchange with your face-down characters, then return two. Block someone from stealing coins from you.

RATING: 4
I know making those kind of games is pretty hard. I liked the idea behind, but did not really like much the results. Most actions are similar, I would have preferred that each role had a totally different aspect.

The addition of coins could have added a new dimension to the game but it has been limited to assasinate for 3 or for 7. So it is merely a tim delayed action.

Another problem is that if people do not bluff enough, you end up in a situation where the game is boring. One way to increase bluffing would be that guessing out your opponent's role could kill him. This would prevent players from using the same role all the time. If there are no basic actions, that would force players to bluff even more.

Another thing that would give player more options once they lost a life is to use the ability of the revealed card. So that at least they still have the option to play 2 actions.

So great ideas, but I think I would prefer something with more meat or more depth. Maybe a combination of love letter, coup and resistance would be nice.

RATING: 7
A nice roles selection and bluffing game in under 15 minutes.  Takes a few plays to get the hang of the different roles and choices to bluff.  Plays quickly and allows a few good choices. 

[Update] I think it plays well with 4 or 5 players.  With 6, there are not enough cards to allow for sufficient room to bluff or acquire certain roles.  I think that with 6 players, there needs to be four cards of each role/character.  This game should scale for the number of players.  

[Update]  Also, with three players, I would suggest thinning the standard deck by one less card of each character.

RATING: 8
[u]Comments:[/u]
Out of the new crop of social experiement games (i.e. Werewolf/Mafia/etc) this was probably the most interesting of games. Although we played the game wrong I could see what that what was there in the game has a lot more teeth than its cousins. It's sister game Resistence shares much with the paradigm Werewolf developed, but took it a step further. With Coup the designer has stepped out of the hidden team element of the game, turning sneaky and blind play into a game that would seem worthy of the title as "ground breaking" for the genre. I handily enjoyed my 3 plays of the game, again even though we played the rules incorrectly. I went back and saw that error in the gameplay I was taught and now I am even more excited to give this one a go as having to discard a successful challenged character, and draw a new one, totally kicks the game up another notch. 

[u]Updated Comments:[/u]
Yup, as I expected when playing with the correct rules the game gets even better. That is a lot of game in a little package, and at some point I will definitely be picking up a copy of this as it is essential to any gamer's collection.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This has a TON of game in the box for the price--one of the, if not the top game for that metric.  The rules are simple, there are multiple strategies, and it REALLY depends on who you play with how the game will go.  You really have to pay attention to other people and have situational awareness.  There's bluffing and strategy and reading people.

But what's the point in calling someone else out, and risking your hide?  If you call them out successfully then you did something to benefit everyone, not really yourself.  And if you're wrong then you lose a lot.  So that's lame.

Some of the roles seem imbalanced.  Especially the Contessa seems pretty weak--it's only useful at the beginning, for blocking against the Assassin, which itself is the other weakest role.  Once people know you have it, they pin you down.

It does start to get repetitive however; and if you are or aren't good at bluffing then you tend to keep repeating the same strategies.

RATING: 7
A bluffing game where players try and push one another out of the central government with deception, assassins, and coups. Players take actions around the court, while their opponents must guess whether they have the requisite nobles in their influence to take those actions legally. Do you call out your rival and risk losing influence yourself, or can you afford to have him collect the money necessary to launch a coup against you?

RATING: 5
Way too luck based. While not a bad game for its length, I do not understand the huge hype for this game.

EDIT: After 18 plays, my opinions have been unchanged, but let me flesh them out a bit more. While there definitely is thought on how each power interacts with each other and certain powers seem better early/late game, all in all the punishment for calling someone out is extremely high, which while facilitating the bluffing aspect of the game makes it a crapshoot of trying to actually deduce who is until near end game if people play cleverly enough, at which point several players might have already been eliminated. For example, let's say the first 4 players all claim Duke. While impossible, the odds of the last person being a Duke is no lower than any of the previous people.

RATING: 6
I enjoy this game, which seems somewhat at odds with the "6" I've given it. The rating is low because I'm still skeptical of how much skill factors in; luck of the draw or inter-player dynamics can sink you in a hurry no matter what. So, played as a competitive endeavor -- not so good. Played as filler entertainment with great opportunities to yell and/or talk smack with your group -- solid.

I skipped the Kickstarter the first time around because I worried that needing to be familiar with and credibly bluff all 5 roles would be a major speedbump for the AP-prone or casual gamers in my group, but experience shows that this is not at all an issue. It's really simple to teach and play. I'm using the expansion Kickstarter as an excuse to fix that mistake and get a shiny copy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Had a blast playing this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
What could be more fun than deciding what hand you WISHED you had at the start of a game, and playing that until someone calls you on it?

I only don't give Coup a 10 because I have played a lot, and someone with a god-hand who isn't enjoying the game can just tell the truth all the way to victory and then everyone feels disappointed. It is sometimes way too obvious when this is happening.

If somebody does something awful to you and you aren't on your toes with pretending to have the counter, there may be nothing you can do because everybody knows you don't have it. This is part of the game.

Once it gets down to 2 players, it isn't much of a game any more, and usually a foregone conclusion as both hands are known. Nevertheless, I adore the 3 player game with my best friends.

Enough people don't enjoy the theme or style of game-play that it doesn't come out very often. It doesn't have as universal appeal as plain old Skull and Roses.

RATING: 7.7
I really enjoy the ideas presented in this game, and the fact that it's a double-hidden identity game only makes the bluffing and social deduction that much more fun. That being said, I find that the end game can sometimes get very mechanical. You might know 3 turns from the end who will win because they can coup first, and there's nothing you can do except just play out the rest of the game. 

I still enjoy it overall, but I think I prefer Mascarade over Coup, which has similar ideas and mechanics arranged in a slightly different way.

RATING: 8
Game is a great re-invention of BS. Each player gets dealt 2 cards secretly, which give them abilities. By stating who you have (without reveling) you do that ability. Others can call BS, and whoever is wrong loses one of their 2 cards. Last person with cards wins.

RATING: 6
Quick little card game of hidden roles,variable player powers and TONS OF BLUFFING. The bluffing aspect made it hard for our group because some us are born liars and others got no "poker face" and we're at a HUGE disadvantage. Additionally, because of the different abilities of each role, we had to keep checking our cards, which did not help our bluffs. Finally, one in our group decided he would play better by not even looking at his cards... and he won... and broke the game. If your group played this enough to become familiar with the various roles and their abilities you could enjoy this game a lot as you would be able to embrace the crucial bluffing aspect. Our group likes variety and this game would not get played enough for us to get familiar with the roles (especially after our first game night where we witnessed the game get "broken").

RATING: 1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Bluffs and double-bluffs. Coup is a very fun game to play with a bunch of liars (which is everyone, ten seconds into the first round of the game. Yes first round, it's the kind of game you don't play just once and done)!

It's a small compact game where everyone is dealt two cards (lives) from a deck of 18. Six roles, three of each, and everyone has a special action, counteraction. Then on your turn you can do either a role action or one of the generic actions (take money, or coup - which means to pay a whole bunch of money to remove one life from a player of your choice, yes, you can be eliminated). However, you don't have to take the role action of a card in your hand, you can take any role action you'd like - but if someone calls your bluff? If you didn't lie, they lose a life (and a role), and if you did well then you do. Also, if you lied, your action, well that's wasted. Then the next player goes. Repeat until there is only one.

When you play a few rounds, patterns emerge. Everyone (say all six players) claim duke in the first round, and nobody calls anyone out on it and so on. Someone hangs back, so they won't be the target of the first wave of assassinations, someone is mercilessly picked off first (last rounds winner maybe) in a collective assassination effort. It's very chaotic, but also very fun. The tension between hanging back, afraid to call someone out and risk one of your own lives, but if noone else is calling them out, can you really afford to let them have it? And slowly people start losing lives, and are eliminated. Maybe a person goes from top to dead by calling bluff against an assassination, that's two lives and out (one for calling a non-bluff and one for the assassination itself). Then suddenly, around the three or perhaps four player point the game becomes a lot more calculatory. You can see the logical outcome if the players are the roles they claimed. A duke can't beat a captain, so you can't be a duke if he's really a captain, which sucks when you have the duke in hand. He also can't let you be something that blocks the captain, so he has no reason not to call your bluff. So what can you do? Well, nothing most likely .

This whole calculatory end game is something I find very fascinating - because it's a tricky puzzle, very deterministic, but with just enough leeway for you to find that one chain of actions that can lead you to victory. The "loud" and chaotic portion of the game becomes the silence of a high level poker table.

So you have a very fun game in a small package, why is it a 6? Well... it has fallen a bit out of favour - for it's time slot I'm reaching for One Night Ultimate Werewolf, or just pass on it altogether and play a longer game. I have a thing against the shorter games, I don't find myself caring that much about the outcome - so the tension they present doesn't grip me as a much - therefore I need them to be fun, very fun, and the calculatory nature of Coups endgame isn't that fun. Plus, if we're playing it four five times, we have time for Cosmic Encounter instead so why not just?

A good game, just not one I reach for enough to rate higher. Better with the expansion, as the "teams" (they aren't teams, it's just adds another layer of interaction, it brings down the chaos and allows for more "gamey" play) are to my liking.

RATING: 5
Love the idea of this. Quick playing, deduction + bluffing, etc. The stakes are a little high, which is fine if it stays quick, but playing with slower players raises the consequences of being wrong which lowers the bluffing and bluff calling which makes the game longer, so it can easily spiral into stalemates. When this happens, it all comes down to who has the money-making roles (Duke or Captain). The Contessa is especially useless in the end-game when you're down to one role and awaiting a death by coup.

Deserves more plays to see if it comes out in the averages, but individual boring plays do happen and I suspect are quite common.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Bluff and use card powers to be the last player standing.

Plays: 3
Type: Deduction game = a game where you attempt to deduce something through elimination.
Complexity: Light.
# of players: See BGG.
Playing time: 15 mins.
Player interaction: Misdirection, conflict and targeting of other players.
Randomness: Low – initial cards, which don't matter all that much.

Replayability: High, it’s quick so why not go again? Plays very fast and every decision matters.
Fun: Quite fun. Bluffing your card powers is exciting! 
Memorability: Decent for such a quick game. You're playing your opponents more than the board, which makes for a memorable experience. It's over very quickly though, there won't be any stories told.

Thematic integration: Weak. It's an abstract deduction game.
Components: Nothing special.

Who will like this: Gamers that want a filler alternative to The Resistance, without the political discussion.

RATING: 7.5
Picked this up a as kickstarter (another ACT group order) and it does the job of a good filler game in that it plays quickly and is easy to learn, but does involve a bit of interesting decision making. The components are all quite good, and the artwork is excellent, and even comes with extra cards for games I don’t (yet) own. Not sure what the smaller thick cardboard token with the illustration of an alternative inquisitor is for though.

As you’d expect with this sort of game, it plays quick and deadly. One mistake can eliminate you from the game but that’s the nature of it. As such, even though there is a two player variant I’d say that this game is a ‘more players the better’ sort of experience.

Overall, good value and another worthwhile addition to the stable of fillers. It has also seen a bit of use in my lunchtime games group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A cool filler-type game focused on bluffing. You get two cards that give you abilities, but you can pretend you are holding different cards and use abilities as if you had those cards. If you call someone on their bluff and it turns out they lied about their cards then they lose one of their two cards, which is quite deadly. You can also assassinate other players or launch "coups" against them if you have enough gold, which lets you remove their cards from play. If someone loses both their cards they're out of the game. This game ends very quickly but people get excited about it and want to play it again, usually we play at least three times in a row.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
One of my favorite bluffing & deception style games. I've played well over 70 games and I haven't found a person who disliked it in all the groups. After the first game, everyone seems to want to play again. Very highly recommended for a short game. Games can go by in a flash or turn into an intense duel between all the players figuring out just what cards their opponents have. 10/10

RATING: 8.2
I'm very impressed with this game. It feels fast and brutal. It's a micro game, not THAT Much longer than love letter. I included a bunch of decorative small stones in the game (similar to the hearts in love letter) that keep track of how many wins each player has had and that way you can keep replaying until one player has gotten 3 stones or so. Might be a good idea for everyone.

This game is interesting, brutal, plays FAIRLY well with just two players (better than love letter scales to only 2 for example) but it much better with more. You really feel like a conning manipulative government operative. There's a real sense of corruption, house of cards style and fits into the Resistance universe while still maintaining bluffing and secret identities. A great win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Everyone starts out with two identities in front of them in secret.  When its their turn they can take 1 coin, take 2 coins (this may be blocked by one of the characters), stage a coup (for 7 coins, remove one persons' card from game), or use a character power.  The character power will ALWAYS go off UNLESS someone calls BS on you.  If they are right you lose a card, if they are wrong you switch your card and THEY lose a card.  Whomever has a card in front of them at the end wins.

I really am enjoying these quick interesting deduction card games like this and Love Letter.  Have to play more of this.

RATING: 8
Eccellente filler basato sul bluff. Un risicato mazzo di carte, un pugno di monete e si crea un gioco divertentissimo, ideale per pokeristi. E' un gioco di ruolo: ogni giocatore riceve 2 carte con rappresentato in ognuna un personaggio. Ogni personaggio ha un potere speciale (ad esempio l'assassino può ammazzare un altro giocatore); i personaggi non vanno rivelati. A turno ogni giocatore svolge un'azione, non necessariamente abbinata ad un proprio personaggio, questa avrà esito positivo se nessuno dubita della reale identità del personaggio. In caso di 'dubito' se il sospetto è fondato il giocatore scoperto dovrà eliminare un personaggio, ma attenzione, se il sospetto è infondato sarà l'accusatore a perdere una carta personaggio. Vince il giocatore che rimane con almeno una carta. Vi faccio un esempio pratico di una fase di gioco: "Sono il Duca e incasso 3 soldi, io solo il Capitano e te lo impedisco; io solo l'Assassino e ti uccido, io sono la Contessa e sfuggo la morte..." se nessuno dubita potrebbero tutti aver inventanto il personaggio ma aver ottenuto il beneficio ed averla fatta franca...  Geniale, vero?

RATING: 6
Pulled this out for the first time tonight and never put it back. Fantastic bluffing game, so simple too. 2/4/2013

Verdict:

What I Like: Continuous boldfaced lying to your friends. A good amount of game in this box. For a filler, it's something I don't mind pulling out every now and again between games or to kill some time. It's easy to teach and quick to play (~5 min). It's inexpensive, but the components are quite good.

What I Don't Like: Lots of impossible situations can arise for particular players in a game. Kingmaking is here in spades.

RATING: 6
[b]Coup[/b] was traded away in a math trade 9/21/14. (Received For Sale)

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]Coup got Jones Theoried in favor of the much more elegant Cockroach Poker. I have talked about this comparison a lot so I will be brief. Cockroach Poker has very simple rules that allow the players to develop their own play style and meta game which makes Cockroach Poker instantly accessible to everyone. There is nothing tacked on or convoluted. 

As much as I have enjoyed Coup there is still a slight barrier to entry with people who don't game much. In fact even in one of my groups a few people really don't like the "heavier" bluffing games but love Cockroach Poker. Another major gripe I have with this game is that it can be over before it starts which isn't very exciting (and can't happen in Cockroach Poker).[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]Great bluffing game were the meta game develops very quickly. It doesn't take long before you start figuring out how you think other people are playing and then the accusations start flying. I really want the original version, I much prefer that art over the Resistance themed cards.[/i]

RATING: 6
You can have two of the 5 characters, but because they're hidden you can say you're using the power of any one of the 5 characters. If people believe you've got that character, you get to use the power. If they don't they call you, bluff style. If you did have the character, they lose one of their characters. If they called correctly and you didn't have the character, you lose a character. If you lose your 2 characters you lose. Which means the game goes pretty correctly. The powers are mainly around taking money, as once you get to 7 money you can spend it to attempt a kill (which can be blocked by only 1 of the 5 characters). So stopping someone getting to 7 seems to be the driver for making bluff calls. So the game works, but there doesn't seem to be enough information to call someone and the game seemed to devolve more to guessing than to bluffing. Which seemed disappointing.

RATING: 10
I've played Coup just a handful of times, but have thoroughly enjoyed it each time. It is fast-paced and easy to learn, with an impressive capacity for strategic and deceptive gameplay. Easily played in a casual or a more intense setting.

It only got 8 stars from me entirely out of personal preference. I see no flaw in the game itself, I simply prefer to be able to feel I can accumulate things within a game (ie claiming land and resources in Catan or setting myself up with a nice gun and horse in Bang), and the nature of the game just doesn't accommodate for that. It's brevity and lack of cards "in play" prevents such.

That being said, it is an extremely fun and addicting game, and although I do not own it, I would very much enjoy if I did.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a hidden role card game with a very small deck, a lot like Love Letter.  This one is all about lying, though.  You announce that you are one of the roles in the game, and then perform its ability.  Someone can accuse you of lying, at which point you can reveal that one of your two cards is that role, or be eliminated.  There's also a small economic component to the game that allows you to eliminate someone who doesn't lie--this prevents the game from stalling out.  I'd feel better about this game if the Duke role wasn't so powerful--it seems like obviously the best role, so it really helps if you can use it without lying.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 6
The second play brought into focus the importance of watching the money, as a coup with 7 coins is unavoidable and quite an aggressive move. Also I feel that the game benefits from a regular group/play so that the role effects are ingrained and so less likely to slip up with seemingly tell tale lying. 

I can see newbies floundering among veterans because the former needs to grasp not only the game mechanics but also the metagame involved with the roles and how to best play or convincingly lie your way forward. So unless new players are willing to get over the hurdle of being easy targets and sitting out, then it is an interesting social game. Not a fan of the sci-fi branding, more interested in the political setting of G54. However as an introduction to hidden roles, I suspect Samurai Sword will be more accessible or if bluffing is more the groups thing then Cockroach Poker will be best suitable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This was the London on Board gang's surprise hit of Essen 2012, and it's another minimalist gem, outdoing Love Letter with just 15 cards! The basic schtick is that it's a secret role game where you don't actually have to have the role to use it, you can just lie!

There are three cards for each of five roles, and every player is dealt two cards. On your turn you take one of the actions associated with one of the roles, whether or not you actually have the matching card. Certain roles can also be used to counter someone else's action. So for example, I might say "I use my Captain to extort two of your money" and then my opponent says "Aha, but I use my Ambassador to block your Captain".

At any time, anyone can call bullshit on any role claim. The called player has to show the role if they have it (and they then shuffle it into the deck and draw a new one). The loser of the call loses one of their cards and has to play on with a hand size of one. If you lose another life, you're eliminated. Last man standing wins - the whole thing lasts under 15 minutes. It's also amazingly well produced for a first-time self-publisher. Good card stock, nice art, very clear rules.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Very fast once everyone knows what the five roles do. Pretty easy to teach, but it may take a few quick rounds before new players get comfortable. It has that "one more play" feel and you can go through enough rounds that several people get to win and the player elimination is no big deal.
I like it better than other bluffing games I've played (like The Resistance) because there's a bit more to the mechanics. For instance, there are general strategies to learn and use, like: if you have one card left and someone tries to assassinate you, you should always call their bluff because you have nothing to lose if it turns out that they aren't bluffing.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.496
Fun bluffing game.  You don't assume a role in this game (like The Resistance), you have two cards in front of you which give you two 'special powers.'

Your cards are not known to the other players, so you can claim 'special powers' that you don't actually have.  If someone challenges you and you are bluffing, you have to turn one if your cards face up (if you weren't bluffing, then the challenger needs to turn a card face up).  If both your cards are face up, you lose that round....so don't bluff too much!

Recommended.  Only downside is there is a little bit of a barrier to entry.  It'll take a game or two to get a feel for how to effectively play the game.  But I suppose that is par for an hidden role game.

RATING: 8.5
(08/13/2014) - I had read a little bit about Coup previously, but had never had a chance to play with. I picked it up for the wedding weekend as a light title that accommodates a variety of player counts.

(09/21/2014) - [7] - All things considered, Coup is a decent game. It really needs a relatively high number of players to shine. Less than four players, this game is a 4 out of 10. More than that and I would put it closer to an 8 or 9.

(09/11/2015) - [8.5] - I find my enjoyment of Coup to be very dependent on both group size and group chemistry. RATING: 9

More interesting to me is claiming to have the character that will block your action and put the decision back on you to decide if you should believe them or not! I like how there's some information about who people could have and by using the architect and drawing cards you can know even more about what cards can't be in people's hands. As people lose influence, more and more information is known and bluffing becomes riskier. This is a Love Letter killer for me.
