RATING: 6
Coup is a clever little bluffing game. It has a self-regulating mechanic which stops anyone getting into a runaway lead - other players will just gang up on anyone doing "too well".

Games are quick enough that the player elimination element isn't too annoying. 

I find it's only worth playing in larger groups though. Smaller games lose a lot of the intricacy and strategy. 

Something I'd play between better games, rather than being a go-to game, really.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Everyone's favourite bluffing game that comes with many problems.
First: it tends to stall with 3 player endings when every player can elliminate any other player but then will be elliminated themselves on next turn by third player. There are rules that prevent stalling forever so it ends with unsatisfactory kingmake-ish finish when one player eventually is forced to chose winner among others.
Second: punishment for called bluff or wrongly accusing someone in bluffing is severe and the gain is usually not that big, so you actually better off not bluffing for the most part, which kinda defeats premise of a bluffing game.
Third: calling out bluffs for non-targetted actions is not motivated at all. In best case scenario you hurt one player, which helps your other opponents. In worst case you hurt yourself. Vigilance isn't rewarded, which also feels off to me.
Fourth: player ellimination. Yeah, it's a quick filler and all that, but you are still going to miss on actually playing the game occasionally.
Nevertheless it's still a fun game and I'll still play it. But if choice is mine to make, I'd pick Mascarade instead.

RATING: 8
Our group really enjoyed this game!  You can get by in this game without bluffing at all (for those people who don't like it or are not good at it) and if you want to bluff, there is plenty of opportunities.  It is much more light-hearted than The Resistance so I think it is less likely to create frienemies even after the game ends**. It requires few people to play and the games are pretty quick.  

The thing I did not like was playing it too many times in a row.  We played 4 or 5 games in a row (since it was so quick) and I was pretty bored by the last one.  Perhaps the expansion adds more interest?

**The difference between The Resistance and Coup:  In The Resistance, you have to lie/convince people to their face using words and tactics.  When something goes bad, there is no way to tell who it was.  It creates a high amount of distrust and backstabbing.  In Coup you carry out an action that everyone sees.  These actions can imply that you are a particular character(you don't have to say anything) and others make inferences based on your actions and the statistics of what is in play to decide whether or not you are bluffing.  In other words, in The Resistance you tell lies and in Coup you bluff (like poker).  Coup is a lot less confrontational but it can cause more revenge plays (because you know who did what).

RATING: 6
My first few months with Coup were revelatory, the first short game I had really enjoyed in years. After several years my enthusiasm has waned significantly. There are a lot of really fun considerations to be made here, but I feel like there are two big problems with the design. First of all, I feel like it is altogether too rewarding for people who don't bluff at all. Secondly, the game really loses steam in the final turns, as play becomes more analytical and less ambiguous. That feels like the opposite of what the arc should be.

I sometimes wonder if Coup's success  is owing to it playing a little like a game of hidden information for people who don't like hidden information in the first place. For me it was a fun honeymoon, but I don't feel like I need to play again.

RATING: 8
When there's five or six players, well, that's too many for Love Letter, so Coup is a great little game to bring to the table if you have ten minutes to spare. Lots of bluffing and goading, this one really shines with parents, grandparents, infrequent gamers and seasoned gamers alike.

Once you've got a few plays under the belt you'll find you can read so much information by the actions or counteractions (or lack thereof), that players take. Inevitably as players reel off abilities, an Ambassador swap or two reveals a bit more information and money becomes stockpiled, players break one by one and the challenges come thick and fast as more and more information is revealed. Character reveals can be a hoot as you find out what some players have been up to.

Good fun and it's over in less than ten minutes. It's always tempting to play just one more round. Plays best with five or six, partly because more cards out results in a little more finesse in the deduction department.

RATING: 7
@Budapest
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Coup fits a rare niche: the quick 5- or 6-player game. Though turns go so quickly that I wish I had more time to think (but hesitation can be a giveaway). The essence of the BS or Liar's Dice game in a very pure, quick-playing form.

End-game with 2-3 people feels clunky compared to the rest of the game. Seems like with 3, first to make a move loses, while with 2, there's a set menu of end-game options to take, and both involve foresight and preparation beforehand.

Cool wow factor on first play, but I don't generally have patience for ultra-quick 'mini-games' (Poker, Werewolf, The Resistance etc) that are more interesting over several rounds where you get a better feel for player tendency (and track wins / winnings).

Challenging seems underpowered since if I Challenge, that gives a really strong signal that I have that role (and more information) but I can't bluff a Challenge either since the penalty is prohibitive. But then if everybody thinks this way, Challenging becomes less predictable/risky... I suppose the game milks its replayability here. Challenge is helped by Ambassador, but is still too weak on the first round. Some foreknowledge would help but difficult to implement that without knowing too much (challenge needs to be a little weaker than bluffing), with how discrete knowledge is in the game.

4+1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Lying, guessing, and getting lucky all within 10 minutes.  The game has great player interaction and is quick and fast paced.  

The downside would be if you can't handle randomness, luck, and potentially getting ganged up on.  What you draw initially will determine a lot of the game.  Still, for how short it is I think it is fine.  This is just not a game that I want to play too much at once because I think it could overstay its welcome.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
There are some games that are long and complicated. And glorious. You play them a few times, trying out a few strategies, eventually internalising the rules, then you move on to the next one.

Then there is Coup. Minimal rules, can be played a half dozen times in a session. And it is glorious.

We play Coup lots. I don't know much about your Coup. I know more than I should ever know about my Coup. We have played so often we are in each others heads.

Terminology and catch phrases fly freely "lay low", "filthy lay low", "Lincoln is a filthy lay low", "sleeper duke", "double contessa", "duke captain", " foreign aid", "me? You are assassinating me??? Why??...contessa", "checks out", "he's consistent", "too big a threat", "best player", "table balance", "too much information", "ruined my deck" etc etc and etc.

This game is like Survivor but in tight little iterations so you can tweak and retweak your strategy multiple times a day. It is everything a short game should be. A masterpiece.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This is the first modern board game I bought and I have played it with many different groups. This game is quick and very fun if the players in the group are willing to bluff.

Since each player only have 2 Influences, calling a bluff is very risky. As soon as you know some players will never lie, there's no incentive to challenge anyone because 99% of time, you'll lose the challenge. Then the game dwindles into whoever has the best cards win, which is very boring. This is the major design flaw of Coup - Not enough incentive to lie to gain an advantage.

If played with the right group though, it's extremely fun. I have had very fun time playing this game.

RATING: 7
Kind of a combination of Poker/Liar's Dice and Love Letter. It's pretty fun for a bluffing game, which I don't typically enjoy all that much. But this is REALLY quick! I kinda want to play it again!

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
The deck (15 cards?) has multiple (3?) copies of each card. Cards are various roles, each associated with a unique power (take money from bank, from another player, kill another person or cancel their power, etc.). Players are dealt 2 at start of game. On your turn, claim to be ANY role and take it's action OR take money from the bank. Anyone can claim you're not actually the role you claimed: if you reveal that you are, they lose a card (and you draw a replacement); otherwise you do. OR, you may spend 7 money to kill one of a player's cards. If anyone loses both their cards, they're out for the rest of the game -- last remaining player wins!

[b]--- Game of Thrones version ---[/b]
You are the head of a great House. You need to manipulate, bluff and bribe your way to power. Your object is to destroy the influence of all the other Houses, forcing them into exile. Only one House will survive...

In Coup, you want to be the last player with influence in the game, with influence being represented by face-down character cards in your playing area.

Each player starts the game with two coins and two influence – i.e., two face-down character cards; the fifteen card deck consists of three copies of five different characters, each with a unique set of powers:

Master of Coin: Take 3 coins from the treasury. Block someone from taking the Royal Grant.
Assassin: Pay 3 coins and try to assassinate another player's character.
Red Priestess: Block an assassination attempt against you.
Smuggler: Take 2 coins from another player, or block someone from stealing coins from you.
Tactician: Draw 2 character cards from the Court (the deck), choose which (if any) to exchange with your face-down characters, then return 2. Block someone from stealing coins from you.

On your turn, you can take any of the actions listed above, regardless of which characters you actually have in front of you, or you can take one of 3 other actions:

Income: Take one coin from the treasury.
Royal Grant: Take two coins from the treasury.
Coup: Pay 7 coins and launch a coup against an opponent, forcing that player to lose an influence. (If you have 10+ coins, you must take this action.)

When you take one of the character actions – whether actively on your turn, or defensively in response to someone else's action – that character's action automatically succeeds unless an opponent challenges you. In this case, if you can't (or don't) reveal the appropriate character, you lose an influence, turning one of your characters face-up. Face-up characters cannot be used, and if both of your characters are face-up, you're out of the game.

If you do have the character in question and choose to reveal it, the opponent loses an influence, then you shuffle that character into the deck and draw a new one, perhaps getting the same character again and perhaps not.

The last player to still have influence – that is, a face-down character – wins the game!

RATING: 4
I like hidden role games when the hidden role mechanic is supplementary to the rest of a good game.  I think when the game IS just hidden roles it often falls flat for me.  Hence I'm not a fan of The Resistance or almost any flavor of Werewolf and this game also falls in that camp.

Each player starts with two hidden roles that have special abilities and each turn players take actions with those characters.  The catch is that a player can claim and take an action with a character they don't have.  The further catch is if you are called out and caught lying you lose one of your characters.  When you lose two you're out and last man standing wins.

There are a couple big problems with this game that I noticed in my first play.  First of all, this game is supposed to be interesting by bluffing and taking advantage of abilities you don't have BUT in my game the players who didn't lie made it to the end since other pleyers would challenge them and knock themselves out.  Also, not challenging and playing it safe also rules out making it even less interesting.  Why risk a character when you have no real way of knowing whether a player is lying or not.  And that's another big problem with this game, there's no way to actually know if someone is lying or not.  Finally, the game is often determined well before the end of the game when everyone does know what each other has and one player has a better hand than another.

It is small, easy to teach, and plays pretty quickly so it has some small value as a filler but there are better fillers and these kinds of games simply aren't for me.

RATING: 7
Because of the speed of this game, I think I've played more Coup than any other game on this list.  Coup is part of the Dystopian Universe, in which The Resistance is also set.  A lot of fun can come from these 15 cards with 5 types of "influences" (the vanilla version without the Reformation expansion, that is).  You start each game with two cards which can be any of five types (Dukes, Captains, Ambassadors, Contessas, or Assassins) and two coins.  The game is all about knowing when to bluff and when to challenge.  Warning:  there is an app for the game, but it cannot capture the face-to-face bluffing spirit that is central to the game (also the app doesn't allow you to draw Foreign Aid).  Get the hard copy of this one, and you won't be disappointed.

RATING: 4.5
This is an okay addition to the rapidly expanding family of "hidden role" games.  In its favour, it's very easy to learn and plays very quickly.  To its detriment, the art and production values are slightly below average.  Though the theme hardly matters in a game like this, it seems clear from the names of the roles and their associated abilities that this game was designed to be in a medieval or Renaissance setting, yet a weird, futuristic sci-fi setting with slightly bizarre artwork was chosen instead.  To be fair, hardly any time is spent actually looking at the cards, but to me that just means that something not dreadfully boring could have been done with the back of the cards.  But what does that have to do with the actual game?  Not much.  As with most games of this type, my biggest concern is that too much time is spent making "educated guesses" rather than cleverly deducing solid information.  I'd rather play poker.  Game design: 2.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 2/5  Total: 4.5/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Quick playing bluffer with too much guesswork. Players are given two role cards, each with their own powers e.g. the Duke allows you to obtain money, the Assassin allows you to kill one of your opponents' roles, the Contessa allows you to block the Assassin etc, and they all interact with one of the other roles in some way. Each player then attempts to carry out an action by claiming to have one of the role cards. If they go unchallenged, then they are successful (whether they possess the role card or not). If they are challenged and do not posses the card then they lose one of their other cards. If they are challenged and they reveal that they are in possession of the role, then the challenged loses one of their cards. Whoever is the last person with cards remaining wins. This can prove to be a fun game but overall you can't escape the feeling that you are just guessing whether your opponents are bluffing or not, although for a game that generally lasts no more than 10 minutes it's difficult to judge it too harshly.

RATING: 6.5
I'm not entirely a fan of The Resistence, I'm more of a board/pieces/cards/victory points type of person.  Bluffing games are fun, but when playing a game of that caliber, unless you REALLY know the other players, there is little chance that the spies won't win.

So I figured I'd try Coup.  I'd heard it was similar, but with somewhat more structure, akin to Love Letter.  It's a fun game, but gameplay is somewhat limited.  If a player gets a 2 good characters and is honest through the whole game, never calls anyone out, it can be pretty easy to win.  All in all, a good one to pull out now and then, but not an "everyday" game.

RATING: 7
A tightly composed competition. The hidden role game in one of it's more precise incarnations.

It's fun, quick and elegant. I haven't played it enough to speak to long-term balance but the bluffing element makes it a very social game and thus quite changeable form one group to another.

Much like Tokaido it is a game that very quickly becomes one of math rather than theme, but also like Tokaido the lightness of that theme prevents this from being a conflict of interest. The game embraces calculation and is a fantastic way to spend time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is considered a micro-game due to its size and playtime.  A round can reasonably be played by a group of 6 people in 15-20 minutes.  Man this game is good!  Coup is a bluffing game where you are trying to knock out the other players.  The game allows you to bluff and counterbluff to other players in a seamless and straightforward way.  The cards allow for plenty of complexity into how you play your bluff, which may intimidate people who don't play games on their first play.  The game mitigates this very nicely by including player helper cards which detail plainly what each card can do.  I love this game!  Every non-gamer I have played this with has been able to pick up the game quickly, while every gamer I have played with love the interactions and depth.  I'd also say that most non-gamers I have played with acclimate to bluffing games more naturally than most other styles of games.  I highly recommend this little game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
For such a short game, this one packs in a lot. Of all the "micro" bluffing games, this one is my favorite. From such a simple set of rules, and over the course of a very short play-time, there develops a persistent sense of paranoia and urgency. If the game were any longer, I'd either feel bored, or I'd have to quit playing lest all the tension drive me bonkers.

To start the game, everyone gets two cards (just two!) from a short deck. You also take two coins. From there, you can choose an action. The trick is that the action you choose can be based on one of the two cards you are holding -- or not. You can lie, and you'll probably have to if you want to win. No on else can see what you're holding, but if they think you're up to something sneaking, they can call you out on your bluff (or is it?!). Once challenged, you must flip a card over. If you were telling the truth, then the other player is out of luck and must discard a card of his own -- playing the rest of the game a card down. If you were lying, then it's you who lose a card. Play continues around the table like this until everyone but one has lost both of their cards. Last person standing wins the game.

Simple enough, but from that simple premise, along with a working knowledge of what all the cards do, a rich play experience emerges. No game last more than 10-15 minutes, so it's impossible to get a sense of long-term strategy -- there is none. Likewise, the game demands repeat plays, and not just because it's fun (it is), but rather you'll want to give everyone a chance to memorize the various special abilities of each card. Nothing gives away the lie more than an overly long pause as you reference the card in front of you. For all that, however, when the game gets humming, there exists a sense of ominous paranoia. My win rate is terrible, but so is everyone else's. The fact of the matter is that Coup creates a feeling of always being on the brink of loss. Your position can fall apart so quickly, that those fears are justified. And yet, when you win, probably because of all the tension, it feels like an accomplishment each and every time. For such a small and short game, that's something worth experiencing.

The game can devolve as players drop out, and an early elimination can feel arbitrary. However, both of these issues can be accounted for with experience. As with any game that involve lying, losing can feel like a personal affront, but that's where the fun is!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.7
Coup is best with probably four experienced players who are all just on fire with the bluffing. Quick bluffs and quick games don't offer the depth that larger social deduction games do, but it fits it's niche well. Reminiscent in ways of Poker, but faster and more fun and doesn't require you to bet your life savings to get a bit of a kick from it. There is more skill in this than people might initially realise, and while some roles may seem more powerful than others, not always - and essentially, you have all roles available to you; it's just knowing when, where, and most importantly, how to play them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Coup is a very simple game.  You have two cards.  On your turn you either take one of a couple of basic actions, or you claim that you jave a certain role card and take the associated action (whether you have it or not).  Most of the actions involve getting/stealing money.  There's an action that lets you force people to throw away one of their two cards.  There are other roles that let you block things that are done to you.  

So one person will claim that he can steal two gold from you.  It goes around the table giving everyone a chance to claim to be the guy who can stop that from happening, or giving them a chance to challenge that you are the guy who can steal gold in the first place.  And if someone says they're the guy who can stop you, everyone else has a chance to challenge that too.  

If you're challenged, you reveal a card.  If it is the correct role, you win and the other guy has to discard one of his cards.  If not, you do.  When you lose a challenge the discarded card goes to the table (where everyone can see it and count how many of the three cards of each type are visible) and you don't get a replacement.  When you win a challenge you discard the card you revealed (which gets shuffled into the leftover card deck) and you draw a replacement.  

When you are out of cards, you're out of the game... last man standing wins.There's a lot of bluffing and trying to figure out who really has what. It's a fairly neat, short game.

RATING: 5
Not an enjoyable first play - I had high hopes for [thing=131357][/thing], but the player elimination took out (at least for this play) the bluff, since no one really wanted to be out, so they (and I) used the character we had. So, when a challenge came, it was dead in the water, since the person always had what they said they had.

I thought [thing=131357][/thing] could be a more focused option of game than [thing=139030][/thing], a game I liked, but when I played with more players and not really gamers, the game went nowhere, since very few were paying attention to what was going on. But no - all [thing=131357][/thing] came to be was a way less fun [thing=139030][/thing], and with player elimination, which though it wasn't really a big issue (as the game is fast), it was the reason why everyone was mostly playing safe. The end game was bad: two players were face to ace, one had an Assassin and the other the Contessa, none would let go of them, and the game turned into a simple and deterministic grab-money-to-kill, sine both knew the other do not had the Embassador to stop them from taking 2 money, and the player with the Contessa could do nothing to slow the pace of the other in taking money. So he was killed and it was over. The game at the start and by the middle, was OK, but the end was and anti-climactic. :shake: A bad experience.

The luck for [thing=131357][/thing] is that my group has many copies of it (we bought together 6 copies) and, since it is easy to teach and to play, and a game is fast (around 20 minutes), it will surely have more opportunities to make a good impression - so far, it didn't.

Lastly, it needs to be said that the production value of the brazilian edition of the game is superb: the box is sturdy, the cards are of a good stock, the colors are crisp. Really top notch.

***

After 4 more plays, three with 4 players, and 1 with 5 players, the ending in all of them were still pretty deterministic and without any shred of fun. From the start up until the last rounds - good fun, but the end is killing the game for everyone in my group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Coup is about convincing your opponents you're holding a card you actually aren't and calling them out when you think they're doing the same to you. Of course, each other player has a little bit of information hinting at your truthfulness, and as the game goes on, each player learns more. By the time all but two are eliminated, perceptive players can deduce which roles are in play, and the game might as well just end at that point. Fortunately, each game is very short, and chaining games back-to-back introduces bluffing on the meta-game level.

RATING: 8
Good:
-Bluffing & double bluffing
-Calling bluffs
-Easy to count the deck
-A meta-game quickly develops in a group
-Not as skill based as people pretend

Bad:
-Not as skill based as people pretend
-Some people feel uncomfortable lying
-Cards are not balanced
-Everyone says they're the Baron
-Chart makes game appear more confusing
-Often an anti-climatic ending

Overall:
A whole lot is packed into this 15 minute game.  It can give you some of your proudest moments in gaming or it can feel like you died before you could really do anything.  You're brain will be turning the entire time but it never feels stressed or overworked.  The highs make this game worth it as you will be eager to call someone's bluff and try not to give a hint that you were lying through your teeth.  This is on the short list of best fillers on the market.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Played only once, so hard to make a long term judgement on replayability and longevity of this game. I find this game somewhat similar to Love Letter, but certainly more complexity and more bluffing involved. Players try to call each other's bluff over character selection. As more characters are revealed over time and players are eliminated, the ability to bluff gets tougher until only one player is left standing. If playing with a new group of people you hardly know, it is extremely tough to figure out if someone is bluffing. With more plays, you may start to see patterns emerge for specific types of players. This will make educated guesses more likely to hit the mark. However, given that this game is short and there aren't a whole lot of decisions to be made, it hard to play multiple rounds over long periods of time. As it stands, I rate this game higher than I think I will over the long run.

05/2014: Indeed, with a few more plays, Coup has slowly grown stale. While the bluffing and poker faces is occasionally intriguing, the game play has not changed that much. Resistance remains a much better social/deduction game.

RATING: 7
Quick fun filler.  The first few rounds is pure guts and bluffing. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The beauty int his game really is in how quickly is plays. Takes 5 minutes to explain, 10 minutes to play, and then you're cruising through multiple games.  

I never thought I'd like a bluffing game, but this one goes so fast it just doesn't feel like that big of a deal.  The Resistance can be exhausting having to argue your point so much, in Coup you're really just staring someone down briefly, "What you don't think I'm that person? Go ahead and call me on it."

The down side is that the game can definitely run it's course and it can happen sooner than you'd expect because of how quick the game plays.  You'll quickly reach a point where you just run into a formula of how you're going to play each turn.  This s where the Reformation expansion helps out in adding another thin layer of strategy.  There can be some great moments where you're trying to think ahead 5 moves and trick people into setting themselves up to lose.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Coup is a bluffing game and almost nothing else, so if you're not into bluffing, don't waste your time with the game.  If you are into bluffing, though, there's an interesting design here.  You can claim any of eight different actions, five of which are associated with character cards, of which you only ever have two.  Bluffing only ever seems to be a valid tactic early in the game, and then the game has the potential to devolve into situations where you have to be honest, and by then, you might be in a position where it's impossible to beat another player.  Anyone in a good position can just hold on to it and wait it out, and he'll win by attrition.  I also wonder if six is the ideal number of players for the game, since the reserve cards are more limited, which makes it more difficult to bluff as players are eliminated and their cards remain face-up before them.  It's a little annoying, but not terribly so.  It's a lot of fun just watching the game play out after you've been eliminated.

2 plays: 4 players
5 plays: 5 players
13 plays: 6 players
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

If I'm rating the entire game based on that one card, make it a 5.

RATING: 7
2 - [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/tag/6p/user/Vroenis]6 players[/url].
Teaching: +5 minutes.
Mastery: 7 - 15 minutes.
Card box.
[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geektag.php?tags=embassy_%26_outpost&username=Vroenis&action=usertags]Embassy_&_Outpost[/url]

[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/tag/outpost_mobile/user/Vroenis]Outpost mobile[/url].

[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/tag/social/user/Vroenis]Social[/url].

Bluffing.
More bluffing.
Some foregone conclusions but...
Quick wrap-ups often leading to...
Beginning again with bluffing.

Coup is a funny beast. The final result is to differing degrees less interesting than the turn by turn bluffing, as well as the meta-game. Often the conclusion maths-out and isn't interesting, but it resolves so quickly that folks often want to start another game.

That's when Coup truly enters its own, not after a single play, but just playing it again and again with the same group of people. A huge meta-game emerges and folks begin gaming one-another in a big way, and at that point, hilarity often ensues.

RATING: 8
In Coup, player gets two secret character cards each. Characters have special abilities, that can be used each turn. The goal is to eliminate all other players cards, by assasignation or coup. The twist is that you can predent to have any character, and perform that special ability. Other players can challenge your honesty, if you lied about your card you loose a card, if you spoke the truth they loose a card.

RATING: 6.725
Bluffing game; you are dealt cards that tell you what actions you can perform... but they're secret.  You have a list of all actions in the game and you can use any of them... but if you use an action that you don't have a card for and someone calls you out, then you lose one of your cards.  If you call someone out and the do have the card, you lose a card.  Lose both cards and your out of the game! Logical deduction alone won't get you through this game, you will need to have a good sense for when your friends are lying.

RATING: 8
>>> Rating Comments >>>

It's easy to fit this quick bluffing game into a game night!

Each player has hidden influence on two benefactors. On each of their turns, they must take an action from a set of common actions or a set of actions related to each benefactor, invoking their power. You do not need to have influence on the benefactor to take that benefactor's action, but beware, as another character can call you out on a use of a benefactor. If you cannot reveal a truthful influence on the benefactor, you lose one of your two hidden influences!

I probably ought to work on this blurb. Or just note my own thoughts rather than trying to overview the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Me and my friends played so many games of Coup that there's no way to count them all. We would play 6 to 10 hands of Coup whenever we met up for game night and it was the perfect way to get the night started or end on a good note. The bluffing is superb and leads to great laughs when the game is over, which usually only takes about 10 or 15 minutes at the most. After playing a LOT of games, the luster starts to wear a little as some endgames play out the exact same way, due to the math of collecting coins to coup and who has what cards left. However, you'll get so many good games out of this little package, and for the price, it's well worth it.
